      <p><b>Iceland</b> (<a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>: <i lang="is">Ísland</i>; <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Icelandic" title="Help:IPA/Icelandic">[ˈistlant]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Is-%C3%8Dsland.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9f/Is-%C3%8Dsland.ogg" class="internal" title="Is-Ísland.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>)<sup id="cite_ref-official_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-official-16">[d]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic</a> <a href="/wiki/Island_country" title="Island country">island country</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">North Atlantic Ocean</a>, with a population of 356,991<sup id="cite_ref-Population_by_municipality_2020_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Population_by_municipality_2020-8">[6]</a></sup> and an area of 103,000&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (40,000&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi), making it the most <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_population_density" title="List of countries and dependencies by population density">sparsely populated country</a> in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[e]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Statice_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Statice-18">[13]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Capital_city" title="Capital city">capital</a> and largest city is <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk" title="Reykjavík">Reykjavík</a>. Reykjavík and the surrounding areas in the southwest of the country are home to over two-thirds of the population. Iceland is <a href="/wiki/High_island" title="High island">volcanically</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_gradient#Heat_flow" title="Geothermal gradient">geologically active</a>. The interior consists of a plateau characterised by sand and <a href="/wiki/Lava_field" title="Lava field">lava fields</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mountain" title="Mountain">mountains</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a>, and many glacial rivers flow to the sea through the lowlands. Iceland is warmed by the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a> and has a temperate climate, despite a high latitude just outside the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a>. Its high latitude and marine influence keep summers chilly, with most of the <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelago</a> having a <a href="/wiki/Polar_climate" title="Polar climate">polar climate</a>. </p>
      <p>According to the ancient manuscript <i><a href="/wiki/Landn%C3%A1mab%C3%B3k" title="Landnámabók">Landnámabók</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_Iceland" title="Settlement of Iceland">settlement of Iceland</a> began in 874 AD when the <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norwegian</a> chieftain <a href="/wiki/Ing%C3%B3lfr_Arnarson" title="Ingólfr Arnarson">Ingólfr Arnarson</a> became the first permanent settler on the island.<sup id="cite_ref-tomasson_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tomasson-19">[14]</a></sup> In the following centuries, <a href="/wiki/Norwegians" title="Norwegians">Norwegians</a>, and to a lesser extent other <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavians</a>, emigrated to Iceland, bringing with them <a href="/wiki/Thrall" title="Thrall">thralls</a> (i.e., slaves or serfs) of <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> origin. </p>
      <p>The island was governed as an <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Commonwealth" title="Icelandic Commonwealth">independent commonwealth</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Althing" title="Althing">Althing</a>, one of the world's oldest functioning legislative assemblies. Following a <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Sturlungs" title="Age of the Sturlungs">period of civil strife</a>, Iceland acceded to Norwegian rule in the 13th century. The establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Kalmar_Union" title="Kalmar Union">Kalmar Union</a> in 1397 united the kingdoms of Norway, Denmark, and Sweden. Iceland thus followed Norway's integration into that union, coming under Danish rule after Sweden's secession from the union in 1523. Although the <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Danish kingdom</a> introduced <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a> forcefully in 1550, Iceland remained a distant semi-colonial territory in which Danish institutions and infrastructures were conspicuous by their absence.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>In the wake of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, Iceland's struggle for independence took form and culminated in <a href="/wiki/Danish%E2%80%93Icelandic_Act_of_Union" title="Danish–Icelandic Act of Union">independence in 1918</a> and the founding of a <a href="/wiki/1944_Icelandic_constitutional_referendum" title="1944 Icelandic constitutional referendum">republic in 1944</a>. Although its parliament (Althing) was suspended from 1799 to 1845, the island republic has been credited with sustaining the world's oldest and longest-running parliament. </p>
      <p>Until the 20th century, Iceland relied largely on subsistence fishing and agriculture. Industrialisation of the fisheries and <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> aid following <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> brought prosperity, and Iceland became one of the wealthiest and most developed nations in the world. It became a part of the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a> in 1994; this further diversified the economy into sectors such as finance, biotechnology, and manufacturing. </p>
      <p>Iceland has a <a href="/wiki/Market_economy" title="Market economy">market economy</a> with relatively low taxes, compared to other <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> countries,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[15]</a></sup> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Trade_union#Prevalence_worldwide" title="Trade union">highest trade union membership in the world</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ILO_Density_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ILO_Density-21">[16]</a></sup> It maintains a <a href="/wiki/Nordic_model" title="Nordic model">Nordic social welfare system</a> that provides <a href="/wiki/Universal_health_care" title="Universal health care">universal health care</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tertiary_education" title="Tertiary education">tertiary education</a> for its citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[17]</a></sup> Iceland ranks high in economic, <a href="/wiki/Democracy_Index" title="Democracy Index">democratic</a>, and social stability, as well as <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_inequality-adjusted_HDI" title="List of countries by inequality-adjusted HDI">equality</a>, ranking third in the world by <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_wealth_per_adult" title="List of countries by wealth per adult">median wealth per adult</a>. In 2020, it was ranked as the fourth-most developed country in the world by the <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">United Nations' Human Development Index</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-UNDP2020_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNDP2020-23">[18]</a></sup> and it ranks first on the <a href="/wiki/Global_Peace_Index" title="Global Peace Index">Global Peace Index</a>. Iceland runs almost completely on <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>. </p>
      <p>Hit hard by the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008" title="Financial crisis of 2007–2008">worldwide financial crisis</a>, the nation's entire banking system <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932011_Icelandic_financial_crisis" title="2008–2011 Icelandic financial crisis">systemically failed</a> in October 2008, leading to an economic crisis<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[19]</a></sup> and the collapse of the country's three largest banks.<sup id="cite_ref-CapitalControlsLifted_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CapitalControlsLifted-25">[20]</a></sup> The crisis prompted substantial <a href="/wiki/2009_Icelandic_financial_crisis_protests" title="2009 Icelandic financial crisis protests">political unrest</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[21]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Icesave_dispute" title="Icesave dispute">Icesave dispute</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[22]</a></sup> and the institution of <a href="/wiki/Capital_control" title="Capital control">capital controls</a> (imposed in 2008 and lifted in 2017).<sup id="cite_ref-CapitalControlsLifted_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CapitalControlsLifted-25">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[23]</a></sup> By 2014, the Icelandic economy had made a significant recovery, due in large part to a surge in <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Iceland" title="Tourism in Iceland">tourism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[25]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Icelandic_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Icelandic culture">Icelandic culture</a> is founded upon the nation's Scandinavian heritage. Most Icelanders are descendants of <a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">Norse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> settlers. <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>, a <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">North Germanic language</a>, is descended from <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse#Old_West_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old West Norse</a> and is closely related to <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese</a>. The country's cultural heritage includes traditional <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_cuisine" title="Icelandic cuisine">Icelandic cuisine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_literature" title="Icelandic literature">Icelandic literature</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sagas_of_Icelanders" title="Sagas of Icelanders">medieval sagas</a>. Iceland has the smallest population of any <a href="/wiki/Member_states_of_NATO" title="Member states of NATO">NATO member</a> and is the only one with <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_without_armed_forces" title="List of countries without armed forces">no standing army</a>, with a lightly armed <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Coast_Guard" title="Icelandic Coast Guard">coast guard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[26]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_Iceland" title="Names of Iceland">Names of Iceland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Norsemen_Landing_in_Iceland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Norsemen_Landing_in_Iceland.jpg/220px-Norsemen_Landing_in_Iceland.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Norsemen_Landing_in_Iceland.jpg/330px-Norsemen_Landing_in_Iceland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Norsemen_Landing_in_Iceland.jpg/440px-Norsemen_Landing_in_Iceland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2768" data-file-height="1887"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Norsemen_Landing_in_Iceland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Norsemen landing in Iceland – a 19th-century depiction by <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wergeland" title="Oscar Wergeland">Oscar Wergeland</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Sagas_of_Icelanders" title="Sagas of Icelanders">Sagas of Icelanders</a> say that a Norwegian named <a href="/wiki/Naddodd" title="Naddodd">Naddodd</a> (or Naddador) was the first <a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">Norseman</a> to reach Iceland, and in the 9th century he named it Snæland or "snow land" because it was snowing. Following Naddodd, the Swede <a href="/wiki/Gar%C3%B0ar_Svavarsson" title="Garðar Svavarsson">Garðar Svavarsson</a> arrived, and so the island was then called Garðarshólmur which means "Garðar's Isle".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Then came a Viking named <a href="/wiki/Fl%C3%B3ki_Vilger%C3%B0arson" class="mw-redirect" title="Flóki Vilgerðarson">Flóki Vilgerðarson</a>; his daughter drowned en route, then his livestock starved to death. The sagas say that the rather despondent Flóki climbed a mountain and saw a fjord (<a href="/wiki/Arnarfj%C3%B6r%C3%B0ur" title="Arnarfjörður">Arnarfjörður</a>) full of icebergs, which led him to give the island its new and present name.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-32">[27]</a></sup> The notion that Iceland's Viking settlers chose that name to discourage oversettlement of their verdant isle is a myth.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-32">[27]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div style="clear:both;"></div>

         <div style="position:absolute; top:0px; width:100%">
          <div style="margin-left:56.582388840453%; margin-top:0; position:relative">
           <span style="position:relative; top:0.25em; left:-1.5px">&nbsp;</span><span style="font-size:smaller; position:relative; line-height:3px; overflow:visible;left:0em; top:0em; z-index:997">1523</span>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div style="position:absolute; top:0px; width:100%">
          <div style="margin-left:81.952920662598%; margin-top:0; position:relative">
           <span style="position:relative; top:0.25em; left:-1.5px">&nbsp;</span><span style="font-size:smaller; position:relative; line-height:3px; overflow:visible;left:0em; top:0em; z-index:997">1814</span>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div style="position:absolute; top:0px; width:100%">
          <div style="margin-left:87.1839581517%; margin-top:0; position:relative">
           <span style="position:relative; top:0.25em; left:-1.5px">&nbsp;</span><span style="font-size:smaller; position:relative; line-height:3px; overflow:visible;left:0em; top:0em; z-index:997">1874</span>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div style="position:absolute; top:0px; width:100%">
          <div style="margin-left:91.020052310375%; margin-top:0; position:relative">
           <span style="position:relative; top:0.25em; left:-1.5px">&nbsp;</span><span style="font-size:smaller; position:relative; line-height:3px; overflow:visible;left:0em; top:0em; z-index:997">1918</span>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div style="position:absolute; top:0px; width:100%">
          <div style="margin-left:93.286835222319%; margin-top:0; position:relative">
           <span style="position:relative; top:0.25em; left:-1.5px">&nbsp;</span><span style="font-size:smaller; position:relative; line-height:3px; overflow:visible;left:0em; top:0em; z-index:997">1944</span>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div style="clear:left;"></div> 
      <h3><span id="874.E2.80.931262:_Settlement_and_Commonwealth"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="874–1262:_Settlement_and_Commonwealth">874–1262: Settlement and Commonwealth</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_Iceland" title="Settlement of Iceland">Settlement of Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Commonwealth" title="Icelandic Commonwealth">Icelandic Commonwealth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christianisation_of_Iceland" class="mw-redirect" title="Christianisation of Iceland">Christianisation of Iceland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Ingolf_by_Raadsig.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ingolf_by_Raadsig.jpg/220px-Ingolf_by_Raadsig.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ingolf_by_Raadsig.jpg/330px-Ingolf_by_Raadsig.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ingolf_by_Raadsig.jpg/440px-Ingolf_by_Raadsig.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1568" data-file-height="1244"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Ingolf_by_Raadsig.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Ing%C3%B3lfr_Arnarson" title="Ingólfr Arnarson">Ingólfr Arnarson</a> (modern Icelandic: <i>Ingólfur Arnarson</i>), the first permanent Scandinavian settler
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>According to both <a href="/wiki/Landn%C3%A1mab%C3%B3k" title="Landnámabók">Landnámabók</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Dslendingab%C3%B3k" title="Íslendingabók">Íslendingabók</a>, monks known as the <a href="/wiki/Papar" title="Papar">Papar</a> lived in Iceland before Scandinavian settlers arrived, possibly members of a <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-Scottish_mission" title="Hiberno-Scottish mission">Hiberno-Scottish mission</a>. Recent <a href="/wiki/Archaeological" class="mw-redirect" title="Archaeological">archaeological</a> excavations have revealed the ruins of a cabin in <a href="/wiki/Hafnir" title="Hafnir">Hafnir</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Peninsula_(Iceland)" title="Southern Peninsula (Iceland)">Reykjanes peninsula</a>. <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dating" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon dating">Carbon dating</a> indicates that it was abandoned sometime between 770 and 880.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[28]</a></sup> In 2016, archaeologists uncovered a <a href="/wiki/Longhouse" title="Longhouse">longhouse</a> in <a href="/wiki/St%C3%B6%C3%B0varfj%C3%B6r%C3%B0ur" title="Stöðvarfjörður">Stöðvarfjörður</a> that has been dated to as early as 800.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[29]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Swedish Viking explorer <a href="/wiki/Gar%C3%B0ar_Svavarsson" title="Garðar Svavarsson">Garðar Svavarsson</a> was the first to circumnavigate Iceland in 870 and establish that it was an island.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[30]</a></sup> He stayed during the winter and built a house in <a href="/wiki/H%C3%BAsav%C3%ADk" title="Húsavík">Húsavík</a>. Garðar departed the following summer but one of his men, <a href="/wiki/N%C3%A1ttfari" title="Náttfari">Náttfari</a>, decided to stay behind with two slaves. Náttfari settled in what is now known as Náttfaravík and he and his slaves became the first permanent residents of Iceland.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[32]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Norwegian-Norse chieftain <a href="/wiki/Ing%C3%B3lfr_Arnarson" title="Ingólfr Arnarson">Ingólfr Arnarson</a> built his homestead in present-day Reykjavík in 874. Ingólfr was followed by many other emigrant settlers, largely Scandinavians and their <a href="/wiki/Thrall" title="Thrall">thralls</a>, many of whom were Irish or Scottish.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[33]</a></sup> By 930, most <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable land</a> on the island had been claimed; the <a href="/wiki/Althing" title="Althing">Althing</a>, a legislative and judicial assembly, was initiated to regulate the Icelandic Commonwealth. Lack of arable land also served as an impetus to the <a href="/wiki/Norse_colonization_of_Greenland" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse colonization of Greenland">settlement of Greenland</a> starting in 986.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[34]</a></sup> The period of these early settlements coincided with the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">Medieval Warm Period</a>, when temperatures were similar to those of the early 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[35]</a></sup> At this time about 25% of Iceland was covered with forest, compared to 1% in the present day.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[36]</a></sup> Christianity was adopted <a href="/wiki/Christianisation_of_Iceland" class="mw-redirect" title="Christianisation of Iceland">by consensus</a> around 999–1000, although <a href="/wiki/Norse_paganism" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse paganism">Norse paganism</a> persisted among segments of the population for some years afterwards.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[37]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Middle_Ages">The Middle Ages</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Sturlungs" title="Age of the Sturlungs">Age of the Sturlungs</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bolungarvik_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Bolungarvik_02.jpg/220px-Bolungarvik_02.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Bolungarvik_02.jpg/330px-Bolungarvik_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Bolungarvik_02.jpg/440px-Bolungarvik_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1000"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bolungarvik_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><i>Ósvör</i>, a replica of an old fishing outpost outside <a href="/wiki/Bolungarv%C3%ADk" title="Bolungarvík">Bolungarvík</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Commonwealth" title="Icelandic Commonwealth">Icelandic Commonwealth</a> lasted until the 13th century, when the political system devised by the original settlers proved unable to cope with the increasing power of Icelandic chieftains.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[38]</a></sup> The internal struggles and civil strife of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Sturlungs" title="Age of the Sturlungs">Age of the Sturlungs</a> led to the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Covenant_(Iceland)" title="Old Covenant (Iceland)">Old Covenant</a> in 1262, which ended the Commonwealth and brought Iceland under the Norwegian crown. Possession of Iceland passed from the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Norway_(872%E2%80%931397)" title="Kingdom of Norway (872–1397)">Kingdom of Norway (872–1397)</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Kalmar_Union" title="Kalmar Union">Kalmar Union</a> in 1415, when the kingdoms of Norway, Denmark and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> were united. After the break-up of the union in 1523, it remained a Norwegian dependency, as a part of <a href="/wiki/Denmark%E2%80%93Norway" title="Denmark–Norway">Denmark–Norway</a>. </p>
      <p>Infertile soil, volcanic eruptions, deforestation and an unforgiving climate made for harsh life in a society where subsistence depended almost entirely on agriculture. The <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> swept Iceland twice, first in 1402–1404 and again in 1494–1495.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[39]</a></sup> The former outbreak killed 50% to 60% of the population, and the latter 30% to 50%.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[40]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reformation_and_the_Early_Modern_period">Reformation and the Early Modern period</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Reformation" title="Icelandic Reformation">Icelandic Reformation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danish-Icelandic_Trade_Monopoly" class="mw-redirect" title="Danish-Icelandic Trade Monopoly">Danish-Icelandic Trade Monopoly</a>, and <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B3%C3%B0uhar%C3%B0indin" class="mw-redirect" title="Móðuharðindin">Móðuharðindin</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Around the middle of the 16th century, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, King <a href="/wiki/Christian_III_of_Denmark" title="Christian III of Denmark">Christian III of Denmark</a> began to impose <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a> on all his subjects. <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3n_Arason" title="Jón Arason">Jón Arason</a>, the last Catholic bishop of <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B3lar" title="Hólar">Hólar</a>, was beheaded in 1550 along with two of his sons. The country subsequently became officially Lutheran and Lutheranism has since remained the dominant religion. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Gerardi_Mercatoris_Atlas,_sive,_Cosmographicae_Meditationes_de_Fabrica_Mundi_et_Fabricati_Figura_(24696368309).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Gerardi_Mercatoris_Atlas%2C_sive%2C_Cosmographicae_Meditationes_de_Fabrica_Mundi_et_Fabricati_Figura_%2824696368309%29.jpg/220px-Gerardi_Mercatoris_Atlas%2C_sive%2C_Cosmographicae_Meditationes_de_Fabrica_Mundi_et_Fabricati_Figura_%2824696368309%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Gerardi_Mercatoris_Atlas%2C_sive%2C_Cosmographicae_Meditationes_de_Fabrica_Mundi_et_Fabricati_Figura_%2824696368309%29.jpg/330px-Gerardi_Mercatoris_Atlas%2C_sive%2C_Cosmographicae_Meditationes_de_Fabrica_Mundi_et_Fabricati_Figura_%2824696368309%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Gerardi_Mercatoris_Atlas%2C_sive%2C_Cosmographicae_Meditationes_de_Fabrica_Mundi_et_Fabricati_Figura_%2824696368309%29.jpg/440px-Gerardi_Mercatoris_Atlas%2C_sive%2C_Cosmographicae_Meditationes_de_Fabrica_Mundi_et_Fabricati_Figura_%2824696368309%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7780" data-file-height="5160"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Gerardi_Mercatoris_Atlas,_sive,_Cosmographicae_Meditationes_de_Fabrica_Mundi_et_Fabricati_Figura_(24696368309).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A map of Iceland published in the early 17th century by <a href="/wiki/Gerardus_Mercator" title="Gerardus Mercator">Gerardus Mercator</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the 17th and 18th centuries, Denmark imposed <a href="/wiki/Danish%E2%80%93Icelandic_Trade_Monopoly" title="Danish–Icelandic Trade Monopoly">harsh trade restrictions</a> on Iceland. Natural disasters, including volcanic eruption and disease, contributed to a decreasing population. Pirates from several countries, including the <a href="/wiki/Barbary_Coast" title="Barbary Coast">Barbary Coast</a>, raided Iceland's coastal settlements and abducted people into slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[42]</a></sup> A great <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> epidemic in the 18th century killed around a third of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[44]</a></sup> In 1783 the <a href="/wiki/Laki" title="Laki">Laki</a> volcano erupted, with devastating effects.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[45]</a></sup> In the years following the eruption, known as the <a href="/wiki/Mist_Hardships" class="mw-redirect" title="Mist Hardships">Mist Hardships</a> (Icelandic: <i>Móðuharðindin</i>), over half of all livestock in the country died. Around a quarter of the population starved to death in the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[46]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="1814.E2.80.931918:_Independence_movement"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1814–1918:_Independence_movement">1814–1918: Independence movement</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_independence_movement" title="Icelandic independence movement">Icelandic independence movement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fj%C3%B6lnir_(journal)" title="Fjölnir (journal)">Fjölnir (journal)</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1814, following the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, Denmark-Norway was broken up into two separate kingdoms via the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Kiel" title="Treaty of Kiel">Treaty of Kiel</a> but Iceland remained a Danish dependency. Throughout the 19th century, the country's climate continued to grow colder, resulting in mass emigration to the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>, particularly to the region of <a href="/wiki/Gimli,_Manitoba" title="Gimli, Manitoba">Gimli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">Manitoba</a> in Canada, which was sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/New_Iceland" title="New Iceland">New Iceland</a>. About 15,000 people emigrated, out of a total population of 70,000.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[47]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A national consciousness arose in the first half of the 19th century, inspired by <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">romantic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nationalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Nationalist">nationalist</a> ideas from mainland Europe. An Icelandic independence movement took shape in the 1850s under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3n_Sigur%C3%B0sson" title="Jón Sigurðsson">Jón Sigurðsson</a>, based on the burgeoning Icelandic nationalism inspired by the <i><a href="/wiki/Fj%C3%B6lnismenn" class="mw-redirect" title="Fjölnismenn">Fjölnismenn</a></i> and other Danish-educated Icelandic intellectuals. In 1874, Denmark granted Iceland a constitution and limited home rule. This was expanded in 1904, and <a href="/wiki/Hannes_Hafstein" title="Hannes Hafstein">Hannes Hafstein</a> served as the first <a href="/wiki/Minister_for_Iceland" title="Minister for Iceland">Minister for Iceland</a> in the Danish cabinet. </p> 
      <h3><span id="1918.E2.80.931944:_Independence_and_the_Kingdom_of_Iceland"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1918–1944:_Independence_and_the_Kingdom_of_Iceland">1918–1944: Independence and the Kingdom of Iceland</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iceland" title="Kingdom of Iceland">Kingdom of Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Iceland" title="Invasion of Iceland">Invasion of Iceland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iceland_in_World_War_II" title="Iceland in World War II">Iceland in World War II</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Berwick_(65).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/HMS_Berwick_%2865%29.jpg/220px-HMS_Berwick_%2865%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/HMS_Berwick_%2865%29.jpg/330px-HMS_Berwick_%2865%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/HMS_Berwick_%2865%29.jpg/440px-HMS_Berwick_%2865%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="784" data-file-height="598"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Berwick_(65).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/HMS_Berwick_(65)" title="HMS Berwick (65)">HMS&nbsp;<i>Berwick</i></a> led the British <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Iceland" title="Invasion of Iceland">invasion of Iceland</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Danish%E2%80%93Icelandic_Act_of_Union" title="Danish–Icelandic Act of Union">Danish–Icelandic Act of Union</a>, an agreement with Denmark signed on 1 December 1918 and valid for 25&nbsp;years, recognised Iceland as a fully sovereign and independent state in a <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> with Denmark. The Government of Iceland established an embassy in Copenhagen and requested that Denmark carry out on its behalf certain defence and foreign affairs matters, subject to consultation with the Althing. Danish embassies around the world displayed two coats of arms and two flags: those of the Kingdom of Denmark and those of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iceland" title="Kingdom of Iceland">Kingdom of Iceland</a>. Iceland's legal position became comparable to those of countries belonging to the Commonwealth of Nations such as Canada whose sovereign is Queen Elizabeth II. </p>
      <p>During World War II, Iceland joined Denmark in asserting neutrality. After the <a href="/wiki/German_occupation_of_Denmark" class="mw-redirect" title="German occupation of Denmark">German occupation of Denmark</a> on 9 April 1940, the Althing replaced the King with a regent and declared that the Icelandic government would take control of its own defence and foreign affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-53">[48]</a></sup> A month later, British armed forces conducted <a href="/wiki/Operation_Fork" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Fork">Operation Fork</a>, the invasion and occupation of the country, violating Icelandic <a href="/wiki/Neutrality_(international_relations)" class="mw-redirect" title="Neutrality (international relations)">neutrality</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[49]</a></sup> In 1941, the Government of Iceland, friendly to Britain, invited the then-neutral United States to take over its defence so that Britain could use its troops elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-53">[48]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="1944.E2.80.93present:_Republic_of_Iceland"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1944–present:_Republic_of_Iceland">1944–present: Republic of Iceland</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Scylla-Odinn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Scylla-Odinn.jpg/220px-Scylla-Odinn.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Scylla-Odinn.jpg/330px-Scylla-Odinn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Scylla-Odinn.jpg/440px-Scylla-Odinn.jpg 2x" data-file-width="902" data-file-height="547"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Scylla-Odinn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>British and Icelandic vessels collide in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Cod_Wars" title="Cod Wars">Cod Wars</a> (Icelandic vessel is shown on the left; the British vessel is on the right)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_constitutional_referendum,_1944" class="mw-redirect" title="Icelandic constitutional referendum, 1944">Icelandic constitutional referendum, 1944</a>; <a href="/wiki/Iceland_in_the_Cold_War" title="Iceland in the Cold War">Iceland in the Cold War</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Cod_Wars" title="Cod Wars">Cod Wars</a>
      </div> 
      <p>On 31 December 1943, the <a href="/wiki/Danish%E2%80%93Icelandic_Act_of_Union" title="Danish–Icelandic Act of Union">Danish–Icelandic Act of Union</a> expired after 25&nbsp;years. Beginning on 20 May 1944, Icelanders voted in a four-day plebiscite on whether to terminate the personal union with Denmark, abolish the monarchy, and establish a republic. The vote was 97% to end the union, and 95% in favour of the new republican constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[50]</a></sup> Iceland formally became a republic on 17 June 1944, with <a href="/wiki/Sveinn_Bj%C3%B6rnsson" title="Sveinn Björnsson">Sveinn Björnsson</a> as its first president. </p>
      <p>In 1946, the US Defence Force Allied left Iceland. The nation formally became a member of <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> on 30 March 1949, amid <a href="/wiki/1949_anti-NATO_riot_in_Iceland" title="1949 anti-NATO riot in Iceland">domestic controversy and riots</a>. On 5 May 1951, a defence agreement was signed with the United States. American troops returned to Iceland as the <a href="/wiki/Iceland_Defence_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="Iceland Defence Force">Iceland Defence Force</a>, and remained throughout the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. The US withdrew the last of its forces on 30 September 2006. </p>
      <p>Iceland prospered during the Second World War. The immediate post-war period was followed by substantial <a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">economic growth</a>, driven by industrialisation of the fishing industry and the US <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> programme, through which Icelanders received the most aid per capita of any European country (at US$209, with the war-ravaged <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> a distant second at US$109).<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[52]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The 1970s were marked by the <a href="/wiki/Cod_Wars" title="Cod Wars">Cod Wars</a>—several disputes with the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> over Iceland's extension of its fishing limits to 200&nbsp;nmi (370&nbsp;km) offshore. Iceland hosted a <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk_Summit" title="Reykjavík Summit">summit in Reykjavík in 1986</a> between United States President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> and Soviet Premier <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a>, during which they took significant steps toward <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_disarmament" title="Nuclear disarmament">nuclear disarmament</a>. A few years later, Iceland became the first country to recognise the independence of <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> as they <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">broke away</a> from the USSR. Throughout the 1990s, the country expanded its international role and developed a foreign policy oriented toward humanitarian and peacekeeping causes. To that end, Iceland provided aid and expertise to various NATO-led interventions in <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_War" title="Bosnian War">Bosnia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kosovo_War" title="Kosovo War">Kosovo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/First_Gulf_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First Gulf War">Iraq</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Iceland joined the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a> in 1994, after which the economy was greatly diversified and liberalised. International economic relations increased further after 2001, when Iceland's newly deregulated banks began to raise massive amounts of <a href="/wiki/External_debt" title="External debt">external debt</a>, contributing to a 32% increase in Iceland's <a href="/wiki/Gross_national_income" title="Gross national income">gross national income</a> between 2002 and 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-jackson_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jackson-59">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[55]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_boom_and_crisis">Economic boom and crisis</span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932011_Icelandic_financial_crisis" title="2008–2011 Icelandic financial crisis">2008–2011 Icelandic financial crisis</a> and <a href="/wiki/2009_Icelandic_financial_crisis_protests" title="2009 Icelandic financial crisis protests">2009 Icelandic financial crisis protests</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In 2003–2007, following the privatisation of the banking sector under the government of <a href="/wiki/Dav%C3%AD%C3%B0_Oddsson" title="Davíð Oddsson">Davíð Oddsson</a>, Iceland moved toward having an economy based on international investment banking and financial services.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-61">[56]</a></sup> It was quickly becoming one of the most prosperous countries in the world, but was hit hard by a <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932011_Icelandic_financial_crisis" title="2008–2011 Icelandic financial crisis">major financial crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-61">[56]</a></sup> The crisis resulted in the greatest migration from Iceland since 1887, with a net emigration of 5,000 people in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-nordregio.se_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nordregio.se-62">[57]</a></sup> Iceland's economy stabilised under the government of <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3hanna_Sigur%C3%B0ard%C3%B3ttir" title="Jóhanna Sigurðardóttir">Jóhanna Sigurðardóttir</a>, and grew by 1.6% in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[58]</a></sup> The centre-right <a href="/wiki/Independence_Party_(Iceland)" title="Independence Party (Iceland)">Independence Party</a> was returned to power in coalition with the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Party_(Iceland)" title="Progressive Party (Iceland)">Progressive Party</a> in the 2013 election.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[59]</a></sup> In the following years, Iceland saw a surge in tourism as the country became a popular holiday destination. In 2016, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Sigmundur_Dav%C3%AD%C3%B0_Gunnlaugsson" title="Sigmundur Davíð Gunnlaugsson">Sigmundur Davíð Gunnlaugsson</a> resigned after being implicated in the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Papers" title="Panama Papers">Panama Papers</a> scandal.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[60]</a></sup> Early elections in 2016 resulted in a right-wing coalition government of the <a href="/wiki/Independence_Party_(Iceland)" title="Independence Party (Iceland)">Independence Party</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Party_(Iceland)" title="Reform Party (Iceland)">Reform Party</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bright_Future_(Iceland)" title="Bright Future (Iceland)">Bright Future</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[61]</a></sup> This government fell when Bright Future quit the coalition due to a scandal involving then-Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Bjarni_Benediktsson_(born_1970)" title="Bjarni Benediktsson (born 1970)">Bjarni Benediktsson</a>'s father's letter of support for a convicted child sex offender.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[62]</a></sup> Snap elections in October 2017 brought to power a new coalition consisting of the Independence Party, the Progressive Party and the <a href="/wiki/Left-Green_Movement" title="Left-Green Movement">Left-Green Movement</a>, headed by <a href="/wiki/Katr%C3%ADn_Jakobsd%C3%B3ttir" title="Katrín Jakobsdóttir">Katrín Jakobsdóttir</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[63]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Iceland.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Map_of_Iceland.svg/330px-Map_of_Iceland.svg.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Map_of_Iceland.svg/495px-Map_of_Iceland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Map_of_Iceland.svg/660px-Map_of_Iceland.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1834" data-file-height="1489"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Iceland.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>General topographic map
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Iceland" title="Geography of Iceland">Geography of Iceland</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Iceland is at the juncture of the North Atlantic and Arctic Oceans. The main island is entirely south of the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a>, which passes through the small Icelandic island of <a href="/wiki/Gr%C3%ADmsey" title="Grímsey">Grímsey</a> off the main island's northern coast. The country lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/63rd_parallel_north" title="63rd parallel north">63</a> and <a href="/wiki/68th_parallel_north" title="68th parallel north">68°N</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/25th_meridian_west" title="25th meridian west">25</a> and <a href="/wiki/13th_meridian_west" title="13th meridian west">13°W</a>. </p>
      <p>Iceland is closer to <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">continental Europe</a> than to mainland North America, although it is closest to <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> (290&nbsp;km, 180&nbsp;mi), an island of North America. Iceland is generally included in Europe for geographical, historical, political, cultural, linguistic and practical reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-Ingebritsen2000_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ingebritsen2000-69">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LehtiSmith2003_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LehtiSmith2003-70">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Miles2005_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miles2005-71">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dhondt2011_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dhondt2011-72">[67]</a></sup> Geologically, the island includes parts of both continental plates. The closest bodies of land in Europe are the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a> (420&nbsp;km, 260&nbsp;mi); <a href="/wiki/Jan_Mayen" title="Jan Mayen">Jan Mayen</a> Island (570&nbsp;km, 350&nbsp;mi); <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">Outer Hebrides</a>, both about 740&nbsp;km (460&nbsp;mi); and the Scottish mainland and <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a>, both about 750&nbsp;km (470&nbsp;mi). The nearest part of Continental Europe is mainland Norway, about 970&nbsp;km (600&nbsp;mi) away, while mainland North America is 2,070&nbsp;km (1,290&nbsp;mi) away, at the northern tip of <a href="/wiki/Labrador" title="Labrador">Labrador</a>. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Icelandic_landscapes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Icelandic_landscapes.jpg/220px-Icelandic_landscapes.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Icelandic_landscapes.jpg/330px-Icelandic_landscapes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Icelandic_landscapes.jpg/440px-Icelandic_landscapes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8192" data-file-height="5568"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Icelandic_landscapes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Three typical Icelandic landscapes
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Iceland is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_by_area" title="List of islands by area">world's 18th-largest island</a>, and Europe's second-largest island after Great Britain. (The island of <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> is third.) The main island covers 101,826&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (39,315&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi), but the entire country is 103,000&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (40,000&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi) in size, of which 62.7% is <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a>. Iceland contains about 30 minor islands, including the lightly populated Grímsey and the <a href="/wiki/Vestmannaeyjar" title="Vestmannaeyjar">Vestmannaeyjar</a> archipelago. Lakes and glaciers cover 14.3% of its surface; only 23% is vegetated.<sup id="cite_ref-NLS_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NLS-73">[68]</a></sup> The largest lakes are <a href="/wiki/%C3%9E%C3%B3risvatn" title="Þórisvatn">Þórisvatn</a> reservoir: 83–88&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (32–34&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi) and <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Eingvallavatn" title="Þingvallavatn">Þingvallavatn</a>: 82&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (32&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi); other important lakes include <a href="/wiki/Lagarflj%C3%B3t" title="Lagarfljót">Lagarfljót</a> and <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BDvatn" title="Mývatn">Mývatn</a>. <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6kuls%C3%A1rl%C3%B3n" title="Jökulsárlón">Jökulsárlón</a> is the deepest lake, at 248&nbsp;m (814&nbsp;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[69]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Geologically, Iceland is part of the <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_Ridge" title="Mid-Atlantic Ridge">Mid-Atlantic Ridge</a>, a ridge along which the <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_crust" title="Oceanic crust">oceanic crust</a> spreads and forms new oceanic crust. This part of the mid-ocean ridge is located above a mantle plume, causing Iceland to be <a href="/wiki/Subaerial" title="Subaerial">subaerial</a> (above the surface of the sea). The ridge marks the boundary between the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_American_Plate" title="North American Plate">North American Plates</a>, and Iceland was created by <a href="/wiki/Rift" title="Rift">rifting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(geology)" title="Accretion (geology)">accretion</a> through volcanism along the ridge.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[70]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Many <a href="/wiki/Fjord" title="Fjord">fjords</a> punctuate Iceland's 4,970-km-long (3,088-mi) coastline, which is also where most settlements are situated. The island's interior, the <a href="/wiki/Highlands_of_Iceland" title="Highlands of Iceland">Highlands of Iceland</a>, is a cold and uninhabitable combination of sand, mountains, and <a href="/wiki/Lava_field" title="Lava field">lava fields</a>. The major towns are the capital city of <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk" title="Reykjavík">Reykjavík</a>, along with its outlying towns of <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B3pavogur" title="Kópavogur">Kópavogur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hafnarfj%C3%B6r%C3%B0ur" title="Hafnarfjörður">Hafnarfjörður</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gar%C3%B0ab%C3%A6r" title="Garðabær">Garðabær</a>, nearby <a href="/wiki/Reykjanesb%C3%A6r" title="Reykjanesbær">Reykjanesbær</a> where the international airport is located, and the town of <a href="/wiki/Akureyri" title="Akureyri">Akureyri</a> in northern Iceland. The island of Grímsey on the Arctic Circle contains the northernmost habitation of Iceland, whereas <a href="/wiki/Kolbeinsey" title="Kolbeinsey">Kolbeinsey</a> contains the northernmost point of Iceland.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Govt_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Govt-76">[71]</a></sup> Iceland has <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_parks_of_Iceland" title="List of national parks of Iceland">three national parks</a>: <a href="/wiki/Vatnaj%C3%B6kull_National_Park" title="Vatnajökull National Park">Vatnajökull National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sn%C3%A6fellsj%C3%B6kull" title="Snæfellsjökull">Snæfellsjökull National Park</a>, and <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Eingvellir" title="Þingvellir">Þingvellir National Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[72]</a></sup> The country is considered a "strong performer" in environmental protection, having been ranked 13th in <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a>'s Environmental Performance Index of 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[73]</a></sup> </p>

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Iceland" title="Geology of Iceland">Geology of Iceland</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Iceland_hotspot" title="Iceland hotspot">Iceland hotspot</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Erupting_geysir.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Erupting_geysir.jpg/220px-Erupting_geysir.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Erupting_geysir.jpg/330px-Erupting_geysir.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Erupting_geysir.jpg/440px-Erupting_geysir.jpg 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="333"></a>

        </div>
       </div>

      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>A geologically young land, Iceland is the surface expression of the <a href="/wiki/Iceland_Plateau" title="Iceland Plateau">Iceland Plateau</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Large_igneous_province" title="Large igneous province">large igneous province</a> forming as a result of volcanism from the <a href="/wiki/Iceland_hotspot" title="Iceland hotspot">Iceland hotspot</a> and along the <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_Ridge" title="Mid-Atlantic Ridge">Mid-Atlantic Ridge</a>, the latter of which runs right through it.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[74]</a></sup> This means that the island is highly geologically active with many volcanoes including <a href="/wiki/Hekla" title="Hekla">Hekla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eldgj%C3%A1" title="Eldgjá">Eldgjá</a>, <a href="/wiki/Her%C3%B0ubrei%C3%B0" title="Herðubreið">Herðubreið</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eldfell" title="Eldfell">Eldfell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[75]</a></sup> The volcanic eruption of <a href="/wiki/Laki" title="Laki">Laki</a> in 1783–1784 caused a famine that killed nearly a quarter of the island's population.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[76]</a></sup> In addition, the eruption caused dust clouds and haze to appear over most of Europe and parts of Asia and Africa for several months afterward, and affected climates in other areas.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[77]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Iceland has many <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">geysers</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Geysir" title="Geysir">Geysir</a>, from which the English word is derived, and the famous <a href="/wiki/Strokkur" title="Strokkur">Strokkur</a>, which erupts every 8–10&nbsp;minutes. After a phase of inactivity, Geysir started erupting again after a <a href="/wiki/2000_Iceland_earthquakes" title="2000 Iceland earthquakes">series of earthquakes in 2000</a>. Geysir has since grown quieter and does not erupt often.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[78]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>With the widespread availability of <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">geothermal power</a>, and the harnessing of many rivers and waterfalls for <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a>, most residents have access to inexpensive hot water, heating, and electricity. The island is composed primarily of <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a>, a low-<a href="/wiki/Silica" class="mw-redirect" title="Silica">silica</a> lava associated with <a href="/wiki/Effusive_eruption" title="Effusive eruption">effusive volcanism</a> as has occurred also in <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>. Iceland, however, has a variety of volcanic types (composite and fissure), many producing more evolved lavas such as <a href="/wiki/Rhyolite" title="Rhyolite">rhyolite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andesite" title="Andesite">andesite</a>. Iceland has hundreds of volcanoes with about 30 active volcanic systems.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[79]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Surtsey" title="Surtsey">Surtsey</a>, one of the youngest islands in the world, is part of Iceland. Named after <a href="/wiki/Surtr" title="Surtr">Surtr</a>, it rose above the ocean in a series of volcanic eruptions between 8 November 1963 and 5 June 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Govt_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Govt-76">[71]</a></sup> Only scientists researching the growth of new life are allowed to visit the island.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[80]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 21 March 2010, a <a href="/wiki/2010_eruptions_of_Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull" title="2010 eruptions of Eyjafjallajökull">volcano in Eyjafjallajökull</a> in the south of Iceland erupted for the first time since 1821, forcing 600 people to flee their homes.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[81]</a></sup> Additional eruptions on 14 April forced hundreds of people to abandon their homes.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[82]</a></sup> The resultant cloud of <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_ash" title="Volcanic ash">volcanic ash</a> brought <a href="/wiki/Air_travel_disruption_after_the_2010_Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull_eruption" title="Air travel disruption after the 2010 Eyjafjallajökull eruption">major disruption to air travel</a> across Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[83]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Iceland_gif.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Iceland_gif.gif/220px-Iceland_gif.gif" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Iceland_gif.gif/330px-Iceland_gif.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Iceland_gif.gif/440px-Iceland_gif.gif 2x" data-file-width="782" data-file-height="532"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Iceland_gif.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>High-field overview of area around Reykir
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Another large eruption occurred on 21 May 2011. This time it was the <a href="/wiki/Gr%C3%ADmsv%C3%B6tn" title="Grímsvötn">Grímsvötn</a> volcano, located under the thick ice of Europe's largest glacier, <a href="/wiki/Vatnaj%C3%B6kull" title="Vatnajökull">Vatnajökull</a>. Grímsvötn is one of Iceland's most active volcanoes, and this eruption was much more powerful than the 2010 Eyjafjallajökull activity, with ash and lava hurled 20&nbsp;km (12&nbsp;mi) into the atmosphere, creating a large cloud.<sup id="cite_ref-connor_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-connor-89">[84]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The highest elevation for Iceland is listed as 2,110 m (6,923&nbsp;ft) at <a href="/wiki/Hvannadalshn%C3%BAkur" title="Hvannadalshnúkur">Hvannadalshnúkur</a> (64°00′N 16°39′W). </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px">
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Iceland_K%C3%B6ppen.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Iceland_K%C3%B6ppen.svg/200px-Iceland_K%C3%B6ppen.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Iceland_K%C3%B6ppen.svg/300px-Iceland_K%C3%B6ppen.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Iceland_K%C3%B6ppen.svg/400px-Iceland_K%C3%B6ppen.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="765" data-file-height="765"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a> types of Iceland
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull.jpeg/200px-Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull.jpeg" decoding="async" width="200" height="134" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull.jpeg/300px-Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull.jpeg/400px-Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="2960" data-file-height="1983"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <a href="/wiki/Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull" title="Eyjafjallajökull">Eyjafjallajökull</a> glacier, one of the smaller <a href="/wiki/Glaciers_of_Iceland" class="mw-redirect" title="Glaciers of Iceland">glaciers of Iceland</a>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Iceland" title="Climate of Iceland">Climate of Iceland</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The climate of Iceland's coast is <a href="/wiki/Subarctic_climate" title="Subarctic climate">subarctic</a>. The warm <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Current" title="North Atlantic Current">North Atlantic Current</a> ensures generally higher annual temperatures than in most places of similar latitude in the world. Regions in the world with similar climates include the <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_Islands" title="Aleutian Islands">Aleutian Islands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Peninsula" title="Alaska Peninsula">Alaska Peninsula</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a>, although these regions are closer to the equator. Despite its proximity to the Arctic, the island's coasts remain ice-free through the winter. Ice incursions are rare, the last having occurred on the north coast in 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[85]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The climate varies between different parts of the island. Generally speaking, the south coast is warmer, wetter, and windier than the north. The Central Highlands are the coldest part of the country. Low-lying inland areas in the north are the most arid. Snowfall in winter is more common in the north than the south. </p>
      <p>The highest air temperature recorded was 30.5&nbsp;°C (86.9&nbsp;°F) on 22 June 1939 at Teigarhorn on the southeastern coast. The lowest was −38&nbsp;°C (−36.4&nbsp;°F) on 22 January 1918 at Grímsstaðir and Möðrudalur in the northeastern hinterland. The temperature records for Reykjavík are 26.2&nbsp;°C (79.2&nbsp;°F) on 30 July 2008, and −24.5&nbsp;°C (−12.1&nbsp;°F) on 21 January 1918. </p>

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plants">Plants</span></h3> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Phytogeographically" class="mw-redirect" title="Phytogeographically">Phytogeographically</a>, Iceland belongs to the Arctic province of the <a href="/wiki/Circumboreal_Region" title="Circumboreal Region">Circumboreal Region</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Boreal_Kingdom" title="Boreal Kingdom">Boreal Kingdom</a>. Around three-quarters of the island is barren of vegetation; plant life consists mainly of grassland, which is regularly grazed by livestock. The most common tree native to Iceland is the northern birch (<i><a href="/wiki/Betula_pubescens" title="Betula pubescens">Betula pubescens</a></i>), which formerly formed forests over much of Iceland, along with aspens (<i><a href="/wiki/Populus_tremula" title="Populus tremula">Populus tremula</a></i>), rowans (<i><a href="/wiki/Sorbus_aucuparia" title="Sorbus aucuparia">Sorbus aucuparia</a></i>), common junipers (<i><a href="/wiki/Juniperus_communis" title="Juniperus communis">Juniperus communis</a></i>), and other smaller trees, mainly willows. </p>
      <p>When the island was first settled, it was extensively forested, with around 30% of the land covered in trees. In the late 12th century, <a href="/wiki/Ari_the_Wise" class="mw-redirect" title="Ari the Wise">Ari the Wise</a> described it in the <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Dslendingab%C3%B3k" title="Íslendingabók">Íslendingabók</a> as "forested from mountain to sea shore".<sup id="cite_ref-Dirt_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dirt-93">[88]</a></sup> Permanent human settlement greatly disturbed the isolated ecosystem of thin, volcanic soils and limited <a href="/wiki/Species_richness" title="Species richness">species diversity</a>. The forests were heavily exploited over the centuries for firewood and timber.<sup id="cite_ref-wildlife_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wildlife-94">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">Deforestation</a>, climatic deterioration during the <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a>, and overgrazing by sheep imported by settlers caused a loss of critical topsoil due to <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>. Today, many farms have been abandoned. Three-quarters of Iceland's 100,000 square kilometres is affected by soil erosion, 18,000&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (6,900&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi) serious enough to make the land useless.<sup id="cite_ref-Dirt_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dirt-93">[88]</a></sup> Only a few small birch stands now exist in isolated reserves. The planting of new forests has increased the number of trees, but the result does not compare to the original forests. Some of the planted forests include <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introduced species</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wildlife_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wildlife-94">[89]</a></sup> The tallest tree in Iceland is a <a href="/wiki/Sitka_spruce" class="mw-redirect" title="Sitka spruce">sitka spruce</a> planted in 1949 in <a href="/wiki/Kirkjub%C3%A6jarklaustur" title="Kirkjubæjarklaustur">Kirkjubæjarklaustur</a>; it was measured at 25.2&nbsp;m (83&nbsp;ft) in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[90]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animals">Animals</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Iceland" title="Wildlife of Iceland">Wildlife of Iceland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Alopex_lagopus_IMG_9019.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Alopex_lagopus_IMG_9019.JPG/220px-Alopex_lagopus_IMG_9019.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Alopex_lagopus_IMG_9019.JPG/330px-Alopex_lagopus_IMG_9019.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Alopex_lagopus_IMG_9019.JPG/440px-Alopex_lagopus_IMG_9019.JPG 2x" data-file-width="5015" data-file-height="3343"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Alopex_lagopus_IMG_9019.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Arctic_fox" title="Arctic fox">Arctic fox</a> is the only indigenous land mammal in Iceland and was the only land mammal prior to the arrival of humans
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Icelandic_sheep_summer_06.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Icelandic_sheep_summer_06.jpg/220px-Icelandic_sheep_summer_06.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Icelandic_sheep_summer_06.jpg/330px-Icelandic_sheep_summer_06.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Icelandic_sheep_summer_06.jpg/440px-Icelandic_sheep_summer_06.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="795"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Icelandic_sheep_summer_06.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>An <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_sheep" title="Icelandic sheep">Icelandic sheep</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Icelandic_Horse_with_blue_eyes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Icelandic_Horse_with_blue_eyes.jpg/170px-Icelandic_Horse_with_blue_eyes.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="251" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Icelandic_Horse_with_blue_eyes.jpg/255px-Icelandic_Horse_with_blue_eyes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Icelandic_Horse_with_blue_eyes.jpg/340px-Icelandic_Horse_with_blue_eyes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4540" data-file-height="6692"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Icelandic_Horse_with_blue_eyes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>An <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_horse" title="Icelandic horse">Icelandic horse</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The only native land mammal when humans arrived was the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_fox" title="Arctic fox">Arctic fox</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-wildlife_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wildlife-94">[89]</a></sup> which came to the island at the end of the ice age, walking over the frozen sea. On rare occasions, bats have been carried to the island with the winds, but they are not able to breed there. <a href="/wiki/Polar_bears" class="mw-redirect" title="Polar bears">Polar bears</a> occasionally come over from <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, but they are just visitors, and no Icelandic populations exist.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[91]</a></sup> No native or free-living reptiles or amphibians are on the island.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[92]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The animals of Iceland include the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_sheep" title="Icelandic sheep">Icelandic sheep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_cattle" title="Icelandic cattle">cattle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_chicken" title="Icelandic chicken">chickens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_goat" title="Icelandic goat">goats</a>, the sturdy <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_horse" title="Icelandic horse">Icelandic horse</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Sheepdog" title="Icelandic Sheepdog">Icelandic Sheepdog</a>, all descendants of animals imported by Europeans. Wild mammals include the Arctic fox, <a href="/wiki/Mink" title="Mink">mink</a>, mice, rats, rabbits, and <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">reindeer</a>. Polar bears occasionally visit the island, travelling on icebergs from Greenland. In June 2008, two polar bears arrived in the same month.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammals" class="mw-redirect" title="Marine mammals">Marine mammals</a> include the <a href="/wiki/Grey_seal" title="Grey seal">grey seal</a> (<i>Halichoerus grypus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Harbour_seal" class="mw-redirect" title="Harbour seal">harbour seal</a> (<i>Phoca vitulina</i>). </p>
      <p>Many species of fish live in the ocean waters surrounding Iceland, and the <a href="/wiki/Fishing_industry" title="Fishing industry">fishing industry</a> is a major part of Iceland's economy, accounting for roughly half of the country's total exports. Birds, especially seabirds, are an important part of Iceland's animal life. <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_puffin" title="Atlantic puffin">Atlantic puffins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skua" title="Skua">skuas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Black-legged_kittiwake" title="Black-legged kittiwake">black-legged kittiwakes</a> nest on its sea cliffs.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[94]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Commercial_whaling" class="mw-redirect" title="Commercial whaling">Commercial whaling</a> is practised intermittently<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[96]</a></sup> along with scientific whale hunts.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[97]</a></sup> Whale watching has become an important part of Iceland's economy since 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[98]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Around 1,300 species of insects are known in Iceland. This is low compared with other countries (over one million species have been described worldwide). Iceland is essentially free of mosquitoes.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[99]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Iceland" title="Politics of Iceland">Politics of Iceland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Politics_Of_Iceland.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Politics_Of_Iceland.svg/450px-Politics_Of_Iceland.svg.png" decoding="async" width="450" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Politics_Of_Iceland.svg/675px-Politics_Of_Iceland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Politics_Of_Iceland.svg/900px-Politics_Of_Iceland.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="450"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Politics_Of_Iceland.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The political system of Iceland
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Iceland has a <a href="/wiki/Left%E2%80%93right_politics" class="mw-redirect" title="Left–right politics">left–right</a> <a href="/wiki/Multi-party_system" title="Multi-party system">multi-party system</a>. Following the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_parliamentary_election,_2017" class="mw-redirect" title="Icelandic parliamentary election, 2017">2017 parliamentary election</a>, the biggest parties are the centre-right <a href="/wiki/Independence_Party_(Iceland)" title="Independence Party (Iceland)">Independence Party</a> (<i>Sjálfstæðisflokkurinn</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Left-Green_Movement" title="Left-Green Movement">Left-Green Movement</a> (<i>Vinstrihreyfingin&nbsp;– grænt framboð</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Party_(Iceland)" title="Progressive Party (Iceland)">Progressive Party</a> (<i>Framsóknarflokkurinn</i>). These three parties form the ruling coalition in the cabinet led by leftist <a href="/wiki/Katr%C3%ADn_Jakobsd%C3%B3ttir" title="Katrín Jakobsdóttir">Katrín Jakobsdóttir</a>.
