Other political parties with seats in the <a href="/wiki/Althing" title="Althing">Althing</a> (Parliament) are the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Alliance" title="Social Democratic Alliance">Social Democratic Alliance</a> (<i>Samfylkingin</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Centre_Party_(Iceland)" title="Centre Party (Iceland)">Centre Party</a> (<i>Miðflokkurinn</i>), Iceland's <a href="/wiki/Pirate_Party_(Iceland)" title="Pirate Party (Iceland)">Pirates</a>, the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Party_(Iceland,_2016)" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Party (Iceland, 2016)">People's Party</a> (<i>Flokkur fólksins</i>), and the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Party_(Iceland)" title="Reform Party (Iceland)">Reform Party</a> (<i>Viðreisn</i>). </p>
      <p>Iceland was the first country in the world to have a political party formed and led entirely by women.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[100]</a></sup> Known as the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_List" title="Women's List">Women's List</a> or Women's Alliance (<i>Kvennalistinn</i>), it was founded in 1983 to advance the political, economic, and social needs of women. After participating in its first parliamentary elections, the Women's List helped increase the proportion of female parliamentarians by 15%.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[101]</a></sup> It disbanded in 1999, formally merging the next year with the Social Democratic Alliance, although about half of its members joined the Left-Green Movement instead. It did leave a lasting influence on Iceland's politics: every major party has a 40% quota for women, and in 2009 nearly a third of members of parliament were female, compared to the global average of 16%.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[102]</a></sup> Following the 2016 elections, 48% of members of parliament are female.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[103]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2016, Iceland was ranked second in the strength of its democratic institutions<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[104]</a></sup> and 13th in government transparency.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[105]</a></sup> The country has a high level of civic participation, with 81.4% voter turnout during the most recent elections,<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[106]</a></sup> compared to an OECD average of 72%. However, only 50% of Icelanders say they trust their political institutions, slightly less than the OECD average of 56% (and most probably a consequence of the political scandals in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_financial_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Icelandic financial crisis">Icelandic financial crisis</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Iceland_–_OECD_Better_Life_Index_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iceland_–_OECD_Better_Life_Index-112">[107]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Iceland" class="mw-redirect" title="Government of Iceland">Government of Iceland</a>
      </div> 
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px">
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Althingishusid.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Althingishusid.jpg/200px-Althingishusid.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="132" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Althingishusid.jpg/300px-Althingishusid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Althingishusid.jpg/400px-Althingishusid.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="463"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           The <a href="/wiki/Althing" title="Althing">Parliament of Iceland</a> in <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk" title="Reykjavík">Reykjavík</a>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Iceland-Reykjavik-Stjornarrad-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Iceland-Reykjavik-Stjornarrad-1.jpg/200px-Iceland-Reykjavik-Stjornarrad-1.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="110" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Iceland-Reykjavik-Stjornarrad-1.jpg/300px-Iceland-Reykjavik-Stjornarrad-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Iceland-Reykjavik-Stjornarrad-1.jpg/400px-Iceland-Reykjavik-Stjornarrad-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3020" data-file-height="1661"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           The <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Iceland" title="Cabinet of Iceland">Cabinet of Iceland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Iceland" title="Prime Minister of Iceland">Prime Minister's Office</a> in <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk" title="Reykjavík">Reykjavík</a>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Law_speaker.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Law_speaker.jpg/170px-Law_speaker.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Law_speaker.jpg/255px-Law_speaker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Law_speaker.jpg/340px-Law_speaker.jpg 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="452"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Law_speaker.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A 19th-century depiction of the <a href="/wiki/Al%C3%BEingi" class="mw-redirect" title="Alþingi">Alþingi</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Commonwealth" title="Icelandic Commonwealth">Commonwealth</a> in session at <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Eingvellir" title="Þingvellir">Þingvellir</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Iceland is a <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democracy</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_republic" title="Parliamentary republic">parliamentary republic</a>. The modern parliament, <i>Alþingi</i> (English: <a href="/wiki/Althing" title="Althing">Althing</a>), was founded in 1845 as an advisory body to the Danish monarch. It was widely seen as a re-establishment of the assembly founded in 930 in the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Commonwealth" title="Icelandic Commonwealth">Commonwealth</a> period, and temporarily suspended from 1799 to 1845. Consequently, "it is arguably the world's oldest <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">parliamentary democracy</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[108]</a></sup> It has 63 members, elected for a maximum period of four years.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[109]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The head of government is the prime minister who, together with the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Iceland" title="Cabinet of Iceland">cabinet</a>, is responsible for <a href="/wiki/Executive_(government)" title="Executive (government)">executive</a> government. </p>
      <p>The president, in contrast, is elected by popular vote for a term of four years with no term limit. The elections for president, the Althing, and local municipal councils are all held separately every four years.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[110]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/President_of_Iceland" title="President of Iceland">president of Iceland</a> is a largely ceremonial head of state and serves as a diplomat, but may veto laws voted by the parliament and put them to a national referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[112]</a></sup> The president is <a href="/wiki/Gu%C3%B0ni_Th._J%C3%B3hannesson" title="Guðni Th. Jóhannesson">Guðni Th. Jóhannesson</a>. </p>
      <p>The cabinet is appointed by the president after a general election to the Althing; however, the appointment is usually negotiated by the leaders of the political parties, who decide among themselves after discussions which parties can form the cabinet and how to distribute its seats, under the condition that it has a majority support in the Althing. Only when the party leaders are unable to reach a conclusion by themselves within a reasonable time span does the president exercise this power and appoint the cabinet personally. This has not happened since the republic was founded in 1944, but in 1942 regent <a href="/wiki/Sveinn_Bj%C3%B6rnsson" title="Sveinn Björnsson">Sveinn Björnsson</a>, who had been installed in that position by the Althing in 1941, appointed a non-parliamentary government. The regent had, for all practical purposes, the position of a president, and Sveinn would later become the country's first president in 1944. </p>
      <p>The governments of Iceland have always been coalition governments, with two or more parties involved, as no single political party has ever received a majority of seats in the Althing throughout the republican period. The extent of the political power possessed by the office of the president is disputed by legal scholars<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (June 2016)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup>, in Iceland; several provisions of the constitution appear to give the president some important powers, but other provisions and traditions suggest differently.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 1980, Icelanders elected <a href="/wiki/Vigd%C3%ADs_Finnbogad%C3%B3ttir" title="Vigdís Finnbogadóttir">Vigdís Finnbogadóttir</a> as president, the world's first directly elected female head of state. She retired from office in 1996. In 2009, Iceland became the first country with an openly gay head of government when <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3hanna_Sigur%C3%B0ard%C3%B3ttir" title="Jóhanna Sigurðardóttir">Jóhanna Sigurðardóttir</a> became prime minister.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[113]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Iceland" title="Administrative divisions of Iceland">Administrative divisions of Iceland</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Iceland is divided into regions, constituencies and municipalities. The eight regions are primarily used for statistical purposes. District court jurisdictions also use an older version of this division.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Govt_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Govt-76">[71]</a></sup> Until 2003, the constituencies for the parliamentary elections were the same as the regions, but by an amendment to the constitution, they were changed to the current six constituencies: </p> 
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li><i><a href="/wiki/Constituencies_of_Iceland" title="Constituencies of Iceland">Reykjavík North</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Constituencies_of_Iceland" title="Constituencies of Iceland">Reykjavík South</a></i> (city regions);</li> 
         <li><i><a href="/wiki/Constituencies_of_Iceland" title="Constituencies of Iceland">Southwest</a></i> (four <a href="/wiki/Enclave_and_exclave" title="Enclave and exclave">non-contiguous</a> suburban areas around Reykjavík);</li> 
         <li><i><a href="/wiki/Constituencies_of_Iceland" title="Constituencies of Iceland">Northwest</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Constituencies_of_Iceland" title="Constituencies of Iceland">Northeast</a></i> (northern half of Iceland, split); and,</li> 
         <li><i><a href="/wiki/Constituencies_of_Iceland" title="Constituencies of Iceland">South</a></i> (southern half of Iceland, excluding Reykjavík and suburbs).</li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl> 
      <p>The redistricting change was made to balance the weight of different districts of the country, since previously a vote cast in the sparsely populated areas around the country would count much more than a vote cast in the Reykjavík city area. The imbalance between districts has been reduced by the new system, but still exists.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Govt_76-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Govt-76">[71]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>74 municipalities in Iceland govern local matters like schools, transport, and zoning.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[114]</a></sup> These are the actual <a href="/wiki/Administrative_division" title="Administrative division">second-level subdivisions</a> of Iceland, as the constituencies have no relevance except in elections and for statistical purposes. Reykjavík is by far the most populous municipality, about four times more populous than <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B3pavogur" title="Kópavogur">Kópavogur</a>, the second one.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Govt_76-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Govt-76">[71]</a></sup> </p> 
      <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed center"> 
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 171.33333333333px">
        <div style="width: 171.33333333333px"> 
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 169.33333333333px;">
          <div style="margin:0px auto;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Iceland_adm_location_map.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Iceland_adm_location_map.svg/254px-Iceland_adm_location_map.svg.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Iceland_adm_location_map.svg/381px-Iceland_adm_location_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Iceland_adm_location_map.svg/508px-Iceland_adm_location_map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1186" data-file-height="841"></a>
          </div>
         </div> 
         <div class="gallerytext"> 
          <p><a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Iceland" title="Regions of Iceland">Regions of Iceland</a> </p> 
         </div> 
        </div></li> 
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 213.33333333333px">
        <div style="width: 213.33333333333px"> 
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 211.33333333333px;">
          <div style="margin:0px auto;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Constituencies_of_Iceland.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Constituencies_of_Iceland.svg/317px-Constituencies_of_Iceland.svg.png" decoding="async" width="212" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Constituencies_of_Iceland.svg/475px-Constituencies_of_Iceland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Constituencies_of_Iceland.svg/634px-Constituencies_of_Iceland.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="291"></a>
          </div>
         </div> 
         <div class="gallerytext"> 
          <p><a href="/wiki/Constituencies_of_Iceland" title="Constituencies of Iceland">Constituencies of Iceland</a> </p> 
         </div> 
        </div></li> 
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 173.33333333333px">
        <div style="width: 173.33333333333px"> 
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 171.33333333333px;">
          <div style="margin:0px auto;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Lower_level_municipalities_of_Iceland.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Lower_level_municipalities_of_Iceland.svg/257px-Lower_level_municipalities_of_Iceland.svg.png" decoding="async" width="172" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Lower_level_municipalities_of_Iceland.svg/385px-Lower_level_municipalities_of_Iceland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Lower_level_municipalities_of_Iceland.svg/514px-Lower_level_municipalities_of_Iceland.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="359"></a>
          </div>
         </div> 
         <div class="gallerytext"> 
          <p><a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Iceland" title="Municipalities of Iceland">Municipalities of Iceland</a> </p> 
         </div> 
        </div></li> 
      </ul> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Barack_Obama_and_the_Nordic_leaders_at_White_House_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Barack_Obama_and_the_Nordic_leaders_at_White_House_01.jpg/220px-Barack_Obama_and_the_Nordic_leaders_at_White_House_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Barack_Obama_and_the_Nordic_leaders_at_White_House_01.jpg/330px-Barack_Obama_and_the_Nordic_leaders_at_White_House_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Barack_Obama_and_the_Nordic_leaders_at_White_House_01.jpg/440px-Barack_Obama_and_the_Nordic_leaders_at_White_House_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2800" data-file-height="1866"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Barack_Obama_and_the_Nordic_leaders_at_White_House_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Nordic prime ministers and the president of Finland visiting the White House in 2016, with Iceland's <a href="/wiki/Sigur%C3%B0ur_Ingi_J%C3%B3hannsson" title="Sigurður Ingi Jóhannsson">Sigurður</a> second from the left
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Iceland" title="Foreign relations of Iceland">Foreign relations of Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Accession_of_Iceland_to_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Accession of Iceland to the European Union">Accession of Iceland to the European Union</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Iceland, which is a member of the <a href="/wiki/UN" class="mw-redirect" title="UN">UN</a>, <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_Free_Trade_Association" title="European Free Trade Association">EFTA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a>, maintains diplomatic and commercial relations with practically all nations, but its ties with the Nordic countries, Germany, the United States, Canada and the other NATO nations are particularly close. Historically, due to cultural, economic and linguistic similarities, Iceland is a <a href="/wiki/Nordic_country" class="mw-redirect" title="Nordic country">Nordic country</a>, and it participates in intergovernmental cooperation through the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Council" title="Nordic Council">Nordic Council</a>. </p>
      <p>Iceland is a member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a> (EEA), which allows the country access to the single market of the European Union (EU). It was not a member of the EU, but in July 2009 the Icelandic parliament, the Althing, voted in favour of application for EU membership<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[115]</a></sup> and officially applied on 17 July 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[116]</a></sup> However, in 2013, opinion polls showed that many Icelanders were now against joining the EU; following the <a href="/wiki/2013_Icelandic_parliamentary_election" title="2013 Icelandic parliamentary election">2013 Icelandic parliamentary election</a> the two parties that formed the island's new government—the centrist Progressive Party and the right-wing Independence Party—announced they would hold a referendum on EU membership.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[118]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_of_Iceland" title="Military of Iceland">Military of Iceland</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Iceland has no <a href="/wiki/Standing_army" title="Standing army">standing army</a>, but has the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Coast_Guard" title="Icelandic Coast Guard">Icelandic Coast Guard</a> which also maintains the <a href="/wiki/Iceland_Air_Defence_System" title="Iceland Air Defence System">Iceland Air Defence System</a>, and an <a href="/wiki/Iceland_Crisis_Response_Unit" title="Iceland Crisis Response Unit">Iceland Crisis Response Unit</a> to support peacekeeping missions and perform paramilitary functions. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Iceland_Defense_Force" title="Iceland Defense Force">Iceland Defense Force (IDF)</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a> <a href="/wiki/Command_(military_formation)" title="Command (military formation)">command</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Armed_Forces" title="United States Armed Forces">United States Armed Forces</a> from 1951 to 2006. The IDF, created at the request of <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>, came into existence when the United States signed an agreement to provide for the defence of Iceland. The IDF also consisted of civilian Icelanders and military members of other NATO nations. The IDF was downsized after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Air Force">U.S. Air Force</a> maintained four to six <a href="/wiki/Interceptor_aircraft" title="Interceptor aircraft">interceptor aircraft</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Station_Keflavik" title="Naval Air Station Keflavik">Naval Air Station Keflavik</a>, until they were withdrawn on 30 September 2006. Since May 2008, NATO nations have periodically deployed fighters to patrol Icelandic airspace under the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Air_Policing" title="Icelandic Air Policing">Icelandic Air Policing</a> mission.<sup id="cite_ref-French_Air_Force_in_Iceland_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-French_Air_Force_in_Iceland-124">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NATO_ACO_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NATO_ACO-125">[120]</a></sup> Iceland supported the <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">2003 invasion of Iraq</a> despite much domestic controversy, deploying a <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Coast_Guard" title="Icelandic Coast Guard">Coast Guard</a> <a href="/wiki/Explosive_Ordnance_Disposal" class="mw-redirect" title="Explosive Ordnance Disposal">EOD</a> team to Iraq,<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[121]</a></sup> which was replaced later by members of the <a href="/wiki/Iceland_Crisis_Response_Unit" title="Iceland Crisis Response Unit">Iceland Crisis Response Unit</a>. Iceland has also participated in the ongoing <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">conflict in Afghanistan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/1999_NATO_bombing_of_Yugoslavia" class="mw-redirect" title="1999 NATO bombing of Yugoslavia">1999 NATO bombing of Yugoslavia</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Despite the ongoing financial crisis the first <a href="/wiki/ICGV_%C3%9E%C3%B3r" title="ICGV Þór">new patrol ship</a> in decades was launched on 29 April 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[122]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Iceland was the neutral host of the historic 1986 <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk_Summit" title="Reykjavík Summit">Reagan–Gorbachev summit</a> in Reykjavík, which set the stage for the end of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. Iceland's principal historical international disputes involved disagreements over <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone" title="Exclusive economic zone">fishing rights</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Conflict with the United Kingdom led to a series of so-called <a href="/wiki/Cod_Wars" title="Cod Wars">Cod Wars</a>, which included confrontations between the Icelandic Coast Guard and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> over British fishermen: in 1952–1956 due to the extension of Iceland's fishing zone from 3 to 4&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">nmi</a> (5.6 to 7.4&nbsp;km; 3.5 to 4.6&nbsp;mi), in 1958–1961 following a further extension to 12&nbsp;nmi (22.2&nbsp;km; 13.8&nbsp;mi), in 1972–1973 with another extension to 50&nbsp;nmi (92.6&nbsp;km; 57.5&nbsp;mi), and in 1975–1976 after another extension to 200&nbsp;nmi (370.4&nbsp;km; 230.2&nbsp;mi).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>According to the 2011 <a href="/wiki/Global_Peace_Index" title="Global Peace Index">Global Peace Index</a>, Iceland is the most peaceful country in the world, due to its lack of armed forces, low crime rate and high level of socio-political stability.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[123]</a></sup> Iceland is listed in the <a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Records</a> book as the "country ranked most at peace" and the "lowest military spending per capita".<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[124]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Akureyri.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Akureyri.JPG/220px-Akureyri.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Akureyri.JPG/330px-Akureyri.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Akureyri.JPG/440px-Akureyri.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Akureyri.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Akureyri" title="Akureyri">Akureyri</a> is the largest town in Iceland outside the <a href="/wiki/Capital_Region_(Iceland)" title="Capital Region (Iceland)">Capital Region</a>. Most rural towns are based on the fishing industry, which provides 40% of Iceland's exports
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Iceland" title="Economy of Iceland">Economy of Iceland</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In 2007, Iceland was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal)">seventh-most productive country in the world per capita</a> (US$54,858), and the fifth-most productive by <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">GDP</a> at <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a> ($40,112). About 85 per cent of total <a href="/wiki/Primary_energy" title="Primary energy">primary energy</a> supply in Iceland is derived from domestically produced renewable energy sources.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[125]</a></sup> Use of abundant <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">geothermal power</a> has made Iceland the world's largest electricity producer per capita.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[126]</a></sup> As a result of its commitment to renewable energy, the 2016 Global Green Economy Index ranked Iceland among the top 10 greenest economies in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-ggei_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ggei-132">[127]</a></sup> Historically, Iceland's economy depended heavily on fishing, which still provides 40% of export earnings and employs 7% of the work force.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Govt_76-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Govt-76">[71]</a></sup> The economy is vulnerable to declining fish stocks and to falls in world prices for its main material exports: fish and fish products, aluminium, and <a href="/wiki/Ferrosilicon" title="Ferrosilicon">ferrosilicon</a>. <a href="/wiki/Whaling_in_Iceland" title="Whaling in Iceland">Whaling in Iceland</a> has been historically significant. Iceland still relies heavily on fishing, but its importance is diminishing from an export share of 90% in the 1960s to 40% in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[128]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Until the 20th century, Iceland was a fairly poor country. It is now one of the most developed countries in the world. Strong economic growth had led Iceland to be ranked <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Human_Development_Index" title="List of countries by Human Development Index">first</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>' <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> report for 2007/2008,<sup id="cite_ref-HDI_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HDI-134">[129]</a></sup> although in 2011 its HDI rating had fallen to 14th place as a result of the economic crisis. Nevertheless, according to the Economist Intelligence Index of 2011, Iceland has the second-highest quality of life in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[130]</a></sup> Based on the <a href="/wiki/Gini_coefficient" title="Gini coefficient">Gini coefficient</a>, Iceland also has one of the lowest rates of income inequality in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-136">[131]</a></sup> and when <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_inequality-adjusted_HDI" title="List of countries by inequality-adjusted HDI">adjusted for inequality</a>, its HDI ranking is 6th.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[132]</a></sup> Iceland's unemployment rate has declined consistently since the crisis, with 4.8% of the labour force being unemployed as of June&nbsp;2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, compared to 6% in 2011 and 8.1% in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Govt_76-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Govt-76">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[134]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Many political parties remain opposed to EU membership, primarily due to Icelanders' concern about losing control over their natural resources (particularly fisheries).<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[135]</a></sup> The national currency of Iceland is the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_kr%C3%B3na" title="Icelandic króna">Icelandic króna</a> (ISK). Iceland is the only country in the world to have a population under two million yet still have a floating exchange rate and an independent monetary policy.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[136]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A poll released on 5 March 2010 by Capacent <a href="/wiki/Gallup_(company)" title="Gallup (company)">Gallup</a> showed that 31% of respondents were in favour of adopting the <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">euro</a> and 69% opposed.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[137]</a></sup> Another Capacent Gallup poll conducted in February 2012 found that 67.4% of Icelanders would reject EU membership in a referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[138]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Iceland_Export_Treemap.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Iceland_Export_Treemap.jpg/220px-Iceland_Export_Treemap.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Iceland_Export_Treemap.jpg/330px-Iceland_Export_Treemap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Iceland_Export_Treemap.jpg/440px-Iceland_Export_Treemap.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2668" data-file-height="2085"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Iceland_Export_Treemap.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Graphical depiction of Iceland's product exports in 28 colour-coded categories
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Iceland's economy has been diversifying into manufacturing and service industries in the last decade, including software production, <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a>, and finance; industry accounts for around a quarter of economic activity, while services comprise close to 70%.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[139]</a></sup> The tourism sector is expanding, especially in <a href="/wiki/Ecotourism" title="Ecotourism">ecotourism</a> and whale-watching. On average, Iceland receives around 1.1&nbsp;million visitors annually, which is more than three times the native population.<sup id="cite_ref-Iceland_–_OECD_Better_Life_Index_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iceland_–_OECD_Better_Life_Index-112">[107]</a></sup> 1.7 million people visited Iceland in 2016, 3 times more than the number that came in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[140]</a></sup> Iceland's agriculture industry, accounting for 5.4% of GDP,<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Govt_76-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Govt-76">[71]</a></sup> consists mainly of potatoes, green vegetables (in <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse" title="Greenhouse">greenhouses</a>), <a href="/wiki/Mutton" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutton">mutton</a> and dairy products.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Govt_76-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Govt-76">[71]</a></sup> The financial centre is <a href="/wiki/Borgart%C3%BAn" title="Borgartún">Borgartún</a> in Reykjavík, which hosts a large number of companies and three investment banks. Iceland's <a href="/wiki/Stock_market" title="Stock market">stock market</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Iceland_Stock_Exchange" class="mw-redirect" title="Iceland Stock Exchange">Iceland Stock Exchange</a> (ISE), was established in 1985.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[141]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Iceland is ranked 27th in the 2012 <a href="/wiki/Index_of_Economic_Freedom" title="Index of Economic Freedom">Index of Economic Freedom</a>, lower than in prior years but still among the freest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[142]</a></sup> As of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, it ranks 29th in the <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a>'s Global Competitive Index, one place lower than in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[143]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/INSEAD" title="INSEAD">INSEAD</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Global_Innovation_Index_(Cornell_University,_INSEAD_and_WIPO)" class="mw-redirect" title="Global Innovation Index (Cornell University, INSEAD and WIPO)">Global Innovation Index</a>, Iceland is the 11th most innovative country in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[144]</a></sup> Unlike most Western European countries, Iceland has a <a href="/wiki/Flat_tax" title="Flat tax">flat tax</a> system: the main personal income tax rate is a flat 22.75%, and combined with municipal taxes, the total tax rate equals no more than 35.7%, not including the many deductions that are available.<sup id="cite_ref-economicfreedom2008_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economicfreedom2008-150">[145]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Corporate_tax" title="Corporate tax">corporate tax</a> rate is a flat 18%, one of the lowest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-economicfreedom2008_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economicfreedom2008-150">[145]</a></sup> There is also a <a href="/wiki/Value_added_tax" class="mw-redirect" title="Value added tax">value added tax</a>, whereas a net <a href="/wiki/Wealth_tax" title="Wealth tax">wealth tax</a> was eliminated in 2006. Employment regulations are relatively flexible and the labour market is one of the freest in the world. Property rights are strong and Iceland is one of the few countries where they are applied to <a href="/wiki/Fisheries_management" title="Fisheries management">fishery management</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-economicfreedom2008_150-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economicfreedom2008-150">[145]</a></sup> Like other <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare states</a>, taxpayers pay various subsidies to each other, but with spending being less than in most European countries. </p>
      <p>Despite low tax rates, agricultural assistance is the highest among OECD countries and a potential impediment to structural change. Also, health care and education spending have relatively poor returns by OECD measures, though improvements have been made in both areas. The OECD <i>Economic Survey of Iceland 2008</i> had highlighted Iceland's challenges in currency and macroeconomic policy.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[146]</a></sup> There was a <a href="/wiki/Currency_crisis" title="Currency crisis">currency crisis</a> that started in the spring of 2008, and on 6 October trading in Iceland's banks was suspended as the government battled to save the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[147]</a></sup> An assessment by the OECD 2011<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[148]</a></sup> determined that Iceland has made progress in many areas, particularly in creating a sustainable fiscal policy and restoring the health of the financial sector; however, challenges remain in making the fishing industry more efficient and sustainable, as well as in improving monetary policy to address inflation.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[149]</a></sup> Iceland's public debt has decreased since the economic crisis, and as of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> is the 31st-highest in the world by proportion of national GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[150]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_contraction">Economic contraction</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932011_Icelandic_financial_crisis" title="2008–2011 Icelandic financial crisis">2008–2011 Icelandic financial crisis</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:W16_Protesters_2659.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/W16_Protesters_2659.JPG/220px-W16_Protesters_2659.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/W16_Protesters_2659.JPG/330px-W16_Protesters_2659.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/W16_Protesters_2659.JPG/440px-W16_Protesters_2659.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2075" data-file-height="1390"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:W16_Protesters_2659.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/2009_Icelandic_financial_crisis_protests" title="2009 Icelandic financial crisis protests">2009 Icelandic financial crisis protests</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Iceland had been hit especially hard by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">Great Recession</a> that began in December 2007, because of the failure of its banking system and a subsequent economic crisis. Before the crash of the country's three largest banks, <a href="/wiki/Glitnir_(bank)" title="Glitnir (bank)">Glitnir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Landsbanki" title="Landsbanki">Landsbanki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kaupthing" class="mw-redirect" title="Kaupthing">Kaupthing</a>, their combined debt exceeded approximately six times the nation's gross domestic product of €14&nbsp;billion ($19&nbsp;billion).<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.1_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.1-156">[151]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[152]</a></sup> In October 2008, the Icelandic parliament passed emergency legislation to minimise the impact of the financial crisis. The Financial Supervisory Authority of Iceland used permission granted by the emergency legislation to take over the domestic operations of the three largest banks.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[153]</a></sup> Icelandic officials, including central bank governor <a href="/wiki/Dav%C3%AD%C3%B0_Oddsson" title="Davíð Oddsson">Davíð Oddsson</a>, stated that the state did not intend to take over any of the banks' foreign debts or assets. Instead, new banks were established to take on the domestic operations of the banks, and the old banks were to be run into bankruptcy. </p>
      <p>On 28 October 2008, the Icelandic government raised interest rates to 18% (as of August&nbsp;2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, it was 3.5%), a move forced in part by the terms of acquiring a loan from <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> (IMF). After the rate hike, trading on the Icelandic króna finally resumed on the open market, with valuation at around 250 ISK per euro, less than one-third the value of the 1:70 exchange rate during most of 2008, and a significant drop from the 1:150 exchange ratio of the week before. On 20 November 2008, the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic countries</a> agreed to lend Iceland $2.5&nbsp;billion.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[154]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 26 January 2009, the coalition government collapsed due to the public dissent over the handling of the financial crisis. A new left-wing government was formed a week later and immediately set about removing Central Bank governor Davíð Oddsson and his aides from the bank through changes in law. Davíð was removed on 26 February 2009 in the wake of protests outside the Central Bank.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[155]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Thousands of Icelanders left the country after the collapse, many of those moving to <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>. In 2005, 293 people moved from Iceland to Norway; in 2009, the figure was 1,625.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[156]</a></sup> In April 2010, the Icelandic Parliament's Special Investigation Commission published the findings of its investigation,<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[157]</a></sup> revealing the extent of control fraud in this crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[158]</a></sup> By June 2012, Landsbanki managed to repay about half of the <a href="/wiki/Icesave_dispute" title="Icesave dispute">Icesave</a> debt.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[159]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_L.P." title="Bloomberg L.P.">Bloomberg</a>, Iceland is on the trajectory of 2% unemployment as a result of crisis-management decisions made back in 2008, including allowing the banks to fail.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[160]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Route1(iceland).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Route1%28iceland%29.png/220px-Route1%28iceland%29.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Route1%28iceland%29.png/330px-Route1%28iceland%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ce/Route1%28iceland%29.png 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="253"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Route1(iceland).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Route_1_(Iceland)" title="Route 1 (Iceland)">The Ring Road of Iceland</a> and some towns it passes through: 1. <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk" title="Reykjavík">Reykjavík</a>, 2. <a href="/wiki/Borgarnes" title="Borgarnes">Borgarnes</a>, 3. <a href="/wiki/Bl%C3%B6ndu%C3%B3s" title="Blönduós">Blönduós</a>, 4. <a href="/wiki/Akureyri" title="Akureyri">Akureyri</a>, 5. <a href="/wiki/Egilssta%C3%B0ir" title="Egilsstaðir">Egilsstaðir</a>, 6. <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B6fn" title="Höfn">Höfn</a>, 7. <a href="/wiki/Selfoss_(town)" title="Selfoss (town)">Selfoss</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Iceland" title="Transport in Iceland">Transport in Iceland</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Iceland has a high level of car ownership per capita, with a car for every 1.5 inhabitants; it is the main form of transport.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[161]</a></sup> Iceland has 13,034&nbsp;km (8,099&nbsp;mi) of administered roads, of which 4,617&nbsp;km (2,869&nbsp;mi) are paved and 8,338&nbsp;km (5,181&nbsp;mi) are not. A great number of roads remain unpaved, mostly little-used rural roads. The road speed limits are 30&nbsp;km/h (19&nbsp;mph) and 50&nbsp;km/h (31&nbsp;mph) in towns, 80&nbsp;km/h (50&nbsp;mph) on gravel country roads and 90&nbsp;km/h (56&nbsp;mph) on hard-surfaced roads.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[162]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Route_1_(Iceland)" title="Route 1 (Iceland)">Route 1</a>, or the Ring Road (Icelandic: <i>Þjóðvegur 1</i> or <i>Hringvegur</i>), was completed in 1974, and is a main road that runs around Iceland and connects all the inhabited parts of the island, with the interior of the island being uninhabited. This paved road is 1,332&nbsp;km (828&nbsp;mi)<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[163]</a></sup> long with one lane in each direction, except near larger towns and cities and in the <a href="/wiki/Hvalfj%C3%B6r%C3%B0ur_Tunnel" title="Hvalfjörður Tunnel">Hvalfjörður Tunnel</a> where it has more lanes. Many bridges on it, especially in the north and east, are single lane and made of timber and/or steel. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Keflav%C3%ADk_International_Airport" title="Keflavík International Airport">Keflavík International Airport</a> (KEF)<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[164]</a></sup> is the largest airport and the main aviation hub for international passenger transport. It serves several international and domestic airline companies.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[165]</a></sup> KEF is in the vicinity of the larger metropolitan capital areas, 49&nbsp;km (30&nbsp;mi)<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[166]</a></sup> to the WSW of Reykjavík center, and public bus services are available.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[167]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Iceland has no passenger railways. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk_Airport" title="Reykjavík Airport">Reykjavík Airport</a> (RKV)<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[168]</a></sup> is the second-largest airport, located just 1.5&nbsp;km from the capital centre. RKV serves general aviation traffic, and has daily or regular domestic flights to 12 local townships within Iceland.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[169]</a></sup> RKV also serves international flights to <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, business and private airplanes along with aviation training. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Akureyri_Airport" title="Akureyri Airport">Akureyri Airport</a> (AEY)<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[170]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Egilssta%C3%B0ir_Airport" title="Egilsstaðir Airport">Egilsstaðir Airport</a> (EGS)<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[171]</a></sup> are two other domestic airports with limited international service capacity. There are a total of 103 registered airports and airfields in Iceland; most of them are unpaved and located in rural areas. The second-longest runway is at Geitamelur, a four-runway glider field around 100&nbsp;km (62&nbsp;mi) east of Reykjavík. </p>
      <p>Six main ferry services provide regular access to various outpost communities or shorten travel distances.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[172]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Wikipedia_and_sources_that_mirror_or_use_it" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="This claim cites another Wikipedia article. Articles need references to reliable third-party sources. (October 2017)">circular reference</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_Iceland" class="mw-redirect" title="Renewable energy in Iceland">Renewable energy in Iceland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg/220px-NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg/330px-NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg/440px-NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1600"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Nesjavellir_Geothermal_Power_Station" title="Nesjavellir Geothermal Power Station">Nesjavellir Geothermal Power Station</a> services the <a href="/wiki/Capital_Region_(Iceland)" title="Capital Region (Iceland)">Capital Region</a>'s hot water and electricity needs. Virtually all of Iceland's electricity comes from renewable resources.<sup id="cite_ref-energy_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energy-178">[173]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewable sources</a>—<a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">geothermal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a>—provide effectively all of Iceland's electricity<sup id="cite_ref-energy_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energy-178">[173]</a></sup> and around 85% of the nation's total primary energy consumption,<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[174]</a></sup> with most of the remainder consisting of imported oil products used in transportation and in the fishing fleet.<sup id="cite_ref-statice1_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statice1-180">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[176]</a></sup> A 2000 report from the University of Iceland suggested that Iceland could potentially convert from oil to hydrogen power by 2040.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[177]</a></sup> Iceland's largest geothermal power plants are <a href="/wiki/Hellishei%C3%B0i_Power_Station" title="Hellisheiði Power Station">Hellisheiði</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nesjavellir_Geothermal_Power_Station" title="Nesjavellir Geothermal Power Station">Nesjavellir</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[179]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1rahnj%C3%BAkar_Hydropower_Plant" title="Kárahnjúkar Hydropower Plant">Kárahnjúkar Hydropower Plant</a> is the country's largest hydroelectric power station.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[180]</a></sup> When the Kárahnjúkavirkjun started operating, Iceland became the world's largest electricity producer per capita.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[181]</a></sup> Iceland is one of the few countries that have filling stations dispensing hydrogen fuel for cars powered by <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">fuel cells</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It is also one of a few countries capable of producing <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> in adequate quantities at a reasonable cost, because of Iceland's plentiful renewable sources of energy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The link between an abundance of renewable energy sources and the ability produce hydrogen cost-efficiently is unclear. (March 2020)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The ranking of geopolitical gains and losses after energy transition (GeGaLo Index) places Iceland first out of 156 countries, making it the main geopolitical winner in the global energy transition.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[182]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Despite this, Icelanders emitted 16.9&nbsp;tonnes of CO<sub>2</sub> per capita in 2016, the highest in EFTA, mainly resulting from transport and aluminium smelting.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[183]</a></sup> Nevertheless, in 2010, Iceland was noted by Guinness World Records as "the Greenest Country", reaching the highest score by the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Sustainability_Index" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental Sustainability Index">Environmental Sustainability Index</a>, which measures a country's water use, biodiversity and adoption of clean energies, with a score of 93.5/100.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[184]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 22 January 2009, Iceland announced its first round of <a href="/wiki/Oil_platform" title="Oil platform">offshore</a> licences for companies wanting to conduct <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon_exploration" title="Hydrocarbon exploration">hydrocarbon exploration</a> and production in a region northeast of Iceland, known as the <a href="/wiki/Jan_Mayen_Microcontinent#Economic_resources" title="Jan Mayen Microcontinent">Dreki area</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[185]</a></sup> Three exploration licences were awarded but all were subsequently relinquished.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[186]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the government of Iceland was in talks with the government of the United Kingdom about the possibility of constructing <a href="/wiki/Icelink" title="Icelink">Icelink</a>, a <a href="/wiki/High-voltage_direct_current" title="High-voltage direct current">high-voltage direct-current</a> connector for transmission of electricity between the two countries.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[187]</a></sup> Such a cable would give Iceland access to a market where electricity prices have generally been much higher than those in Iceland.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[188]</a></sup> Iceland has considerable renewable energy resources, especially geothermal energy and hydropower resources,<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[189]</a></sup> and most of the potential has not been developed, partly because there is not enough demand for additional electricity generation capacity from the residents and industry of Iceland; the United Kingdom is interested in importing inexpensive electricity from renewable sources of energy, and this could lead to further development of the energy resources. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_science">Education and science</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Iceland" title="Education in Iceland">Education in Iceland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk_(main_building,_2004).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk_%28main_building%2C_2004%29.jpg/220px-Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk_%28main_building%2C_2004%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk_%28main_building%2C_2004%29.jpg/330px-Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk_%28main_building%2C_2004%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk_%28main_building%2C_2004%29.jpg/440px-Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk_%28main_building%2C_2004%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk_(main_building,_2004).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Reykjavík Junior College (<i><a href="/wiki/Menntask%C3%B3linn_%C3%AD_Reykjav%C3%ADk" title="Menntaskólinn í Reykjavík">Menntaskólinn í Reykjavík</a></i>), located in downtown Reykjavík, is the oldest <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">gymnasium</a> in Iceland
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Education,_Science_and_Culture_(Iceland)" title="Ministry of Education, Science and Culture (Iceland)">Ministry of Education, Science and Culture</a> is responsible for the policies and methods that schools must use, and they issue the National Curriculum Guidelines. However, playschools, primary schools, and lower secondary schools are funded and administered by the municipalities. The government does allow citizens to <a href="/wiki/Homeschooling" title="Homeschooling">home educate</a> their children, however under a very strict set of demands.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[190]</a></sup> Students must adhere closely to the government mandated curriculum, and the parent teaching must acquire a government approved teaching certificate. </p>
      <p>Nursery school, or <i>leikskóli</i>, is non-compulsory education for children younger than six years, and is the first step in the education system. The current legislation concerning playschools was passed in 1994. They are also responsible for ensuring that the curriculum is suitable so as to make the transition into compulsory education as easy as possible.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Compulsory education, or <i>grunnskóli</i>, comprises primary and lower secondary education, which often is conducted at the same institution. Education is mandatory by law for children aged from 6 to 16&nbsp;years. The school year lasts nine months, beginning between 21 August and 1 September, ending between 31 May and 10 June. The minimum number of school days was once 170, but after a new teachers' wage contract, it increased to 180. Lessons take place five days a week. All public schools have mandatory education in Christianity, although an exemption may be considered by the <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Education,_Science_and_Culture_(Iceland)" title="Minister of Education, Science and Culture (Iceland)">Minister of Education</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jonathan_Fox_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jonathan_Fox-196">[191]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Upper secondary education, or <i>framhaldsskóli</i>, follows lower secondary education. These schools are also known as <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">gymnasia</a> in English. Though not compulsory, everyone who has had a compulsory education has the right to upper secondary education. This stage of education is governed by the Upper Secondary School Act of 1996. All schools in Iceland are mixed sex schools. The largest seat of higher education is the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Iceland" title="University of Iceland">University of Iceland</a>, which has its main campus in central Reykjavík. Other schools offering university-level instruction include <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk_University" title="Reykjavík University">Reykjavík University</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Akureyri" title="University of Akureyri">University of Akureyri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_University_of_Iceland" title="Agricultural University of Iceland">Agricultural University of Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bifr%C3%B6st_University" title="Bifröst University">Bifröst University</a>. </p>
      <p>An OECD assessment found 64% of Icelanders aged 25–64 have earned the equivalent of a high-school degree, which is lower than the OECD average of 73%. Among 25- to 34-year-olds, only 69% have earned the equivalent of a high-school degree, significantly lower than the OECD average of 80%.<sup id="cite_ref-Iceland_–_OECD_Better_Life_Index_112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iceland_–_OECD_Better_Life_Index-112">[107]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Iceland's education system is considered excellent: the <a href="/wiki/Programme_for_International_Student_Assessment" title="Programme for International Student Assessment">Programme for International Student Assessment</a> ranks it as the 16th best performing, above the OECD average.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[192]</a></sup> Students were particularly proficient in reading and mathematics. </p>
      <p>According to a 2013 <a href="/wiki/Eurostat" title="Eurostat">Eurostat</a> report by the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>, Iceland spends around 3.11% of its GDP on scientific research and development (R&amp;D), over 1 percentage point higher than the EU average of 2.03%, and has set a target of 4% to reach by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[193]</a></sup> A 2010 <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> report found that out of 72 countries that spend the most on R&amp;D (100&nbsp;million US dollars or more), Iceland ranked 9th by proportion of GDP, tied with Taiwan, Switzerland, and Germany and ahead of France, the UK, and Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[194]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       For statistics on demographics, see <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Iceland" title="Demographics of Iceland">Demographics of Iceland</a>.
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Icelanders" title="Icelanders">Icelanders</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Reykjav%C3%ADk_s%C3%A9%C3%B0_%C3%BAr_Hallgr%C3%ADmskirkju_6.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Reykjav%C3%ADk_s%C3%A9%C3%B0_%C3%BAr_Hallgr%C3%ADmskirkju_6.JPG/220px-Reykjav%C3%ADk_s%C3%A9%C3%B0_%C3%BAr_Hallgr%C3%ADmskirkju_6.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Reykjav%C3%ADk_s%C3%A9%C3%B0_%C3%BAr_Hallgr%C3%ADmskirkju_6.JPG/330px-Reykjav%C3%ADk_s%C3%A9%C3%B0_%C3%BAr_Hallgr%C3%ADmskirkju_6.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Reykjav%C3%ADk_s%C3%A9%C3%B0_%C3%BAr_Hallgr%C3%ADmskirkju_6.JPG/440px-Reykjav%C3%ADk_s%C3%A9%C3%B0_%C3%BAr_Hallgr%C3%ADmskirkju_6.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3296" data-file-height="2472"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Reykjav%C3%ADk_s%C3%A9%C3%B0_%C3%BAr_Hallgr%C3%ADmskirkju_6.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk" title="Reykjavík">Reykjavík</a>, Iceland's largest metropolitan area and the centre of the <a href="/wiki/Capital_Region_(Iceland)" title="Capital Region (Iceland)">Capital Region</a> which, with a population of 233,034, makes for 64% of Iceland's population. (numbers from 2020)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The original population of Iceland was of <a href="/wiki/Nordic_race" title="Nordic race">Nordic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> origin. This is evident from literary evidence dating from the settlement period as well as from later scientific studies such as <a href="/wiki/Blood_type" title="Blood type">blood type</a> and genetic analyses. One such genetic study indicated that the majority of the male settlers were of Nordic origin while the majority of the women were of Gaelic origin, meaning many settlers of Iceland were Norsemen who brought Gaelic slaves with them.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[195]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Iceland has extensive genealogical records dating back to the late 17th century and fragmentary records extending back to the <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_Iceland" title="Settlement of Iceland">Age of Settlement</a>. The biopharmaceutical company <a href="/wiki/DeCODE_genetics" title="DeCODE genetics">deCODE genetics</a> has funded the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Genealogy" title="Genealogy">genealogy</a> database that is intended to cover all of Iceland's known inhabitants. It views the database, called <i><a href="/wiki/%C3%8Dslendingab%C3%B3k_(genealogical_database)" title="Íslendingabók (genealogical database)">Íslendingabók</a></i>, as a valuable tool for conducting research on genetic diseases, given the relative isolation of Iceland's population. </p>
      <p>The population of the island is believed to have varied from 40,000 to 60,000 in the period ranging from initial settlement until the mid-19th century. During that time, cold winters, ash fall from volcanic eruptions, and <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plagues</a> adversely affected the population several times.<sup id="cite_ref-tomasson_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tomasson-19">[14]</a></sup> There were 37 <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a> years in Iceland between 1500 and 1804.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[196]</a></sup> The first census was carried out in 1703 and revealed that the population was then 50,358. After the destructive volcanic eruptions of the <a href="/wiki/Laki" title="Laki">Laki volcano</a> during 1783–1784, the population reached a low of about 40,000.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[197]</a></sup> Improving living conditions have triggered a rapid increase in population since the mid-19th century—from about 60,000 in 1850 to 320,000 in 2008. Iceland has a relatively young population for a developed country, with one out of five people being 14&nbsp;years old or younger. With a fertility rate of 2.1, Iceland is one of only a few European countries with a birth rate <a href="/wiki/Replacement_fertility_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Replacement fertility rate">sufficient for long-term population growth</a> (see table below).<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[198]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[199]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In December 2007, 33,678 people (13.5% of the total population) living in Iceland had been born abroad, including children of Icelandic parents living abroad. Around 19,000 people (6% of the population) held foreign citizenship. <a href="/wiki/Polish_diaspora" title="Polish diaspora">Polish</a> people make up the largest minority group by a considerable margin, and still form the bulk of the foreign workforce. About 8,000 Poles now live in Iceland, 1,500 of them in <a href="/wiki/Fjar%C3%B0abygg%C3%B0" title="Fjarðabyggð">Fjarðabyggð</a> where they make up 75% of the workforce who are constructing the Fjarðarál aluminium plant.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[200]</a></sup> Large-scale construction projects in the east of Iceland (see <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1rahnj%C3%BAkar_Hydropower_Plant" title="Kárahnjúkar Hydropower Plant">Kárahnjúkar Hydropower Plant</a>) have also brought in many people whose stay is expected to be temporary. Many Polish immigrants were also considering leaving in 2008 as a result of the <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932011_Icelandic_financial_crisis" title="2008–2011 Icelandic financial crisis">Icelandic financial crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[201]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The southwest corner of Iceland is by far the most densely populated region. It is also the location of the capital Reykjavík, the northernmost national capital in the world. More than 70 per cent of Iceland's population live in the southwest corner (<a href="/wiki/Capital_Region_(Iceland)" title="Capital Region (Iceland)">Greater Reykjavík</a> and the nearby <a href="/wiki/Southern_Peninsula_(Iceland)" title="Southern Peninsula (Iceland)">Southern Peninsula</a>), which covers less than two per cent of Iceland's land area. The largest town outside Greater Reykjavík is <a href="/wiki/Reykjanesb%C3%A6r" title="Reykjanesbær">Reykjanesbær</a>, which is located on the Southern Peninsula, less than 50&nbsp;km (31&nbsp;mi) from the capital. The largest town outside the southwest corner is <a href="/wiki/Akureyri" title="Akureyri">Akureyri</a> in northern Iceland. </p>
      <p>Some 500 Icelanders under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Erik_the_Red" title="Erik the Red">Erik the Red</a> settled <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> in the late 10th century.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[202]</a></sup> The total population reached a high point of perhaps 5,000, and developed independent institutions before disappearing by 1500.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[203]</a></sup> People from Greenland attempted to set up a settlement at <a href="/wiki/Vinland" title="Vinland">Vinland</a> in North America, but abandoned it in the face of hostility from the indigenous residents.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[204]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Emigration of Icelanders to the United States and Canada began in the 1870s. As of 2006<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iceland&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Canada had over 88,000 people of <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Canadian" class="mw-redirect" title="Icelandic Canadian">Icelandic</a> descent,<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[205]</a></sup> while there are more than 40,000 Americans of <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Icelandic American">Icelandic</a> descent, according to the 2000 US census.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[206]</a></sup> </p>
